          <div style="clear:right">
          </div>
          <div class="t">
           Fertility of the offspring isn't an exact indicator of what makes two animals part of the same species, but it's very true that the wolf species is really vast. It's amazing how the canis lupus species stretches from gray wolves to dingos and even pomeranians. Conversely, it's funny how wildcats (felis silvestris) are considered to be a whole different species from regular house cats. There are even better examples of differing species that look nearly identical.
          </div>
         </div>
        </div>

         <div class="t withAv">
          For those who have trouble understanding the idea of evolution:
          <br/>
          Saying 'X evolved into Y' is actually a misunderstanding of how evolution works.
          <br/>
          A better way of saying it is 'Species X and Y had a common ancestor, species Z'
          <br/>
          Humans didn't evolve from monkeys (at least not modern monkeys). Humans and other primates share a common ancestor. Think of evolution not as a line, but as a tree.
         </div>
        </div>

          <div class="t withAv">
           <span style="color:#0C3EE3">
            When people say evolution they usually mean natural selection, but evolution is everything that alters the percentage of certain genes in a species. It can come from mutations, climate change or gene flow
            <span class="spoiler">
             <span class="spoiler-icon">
              <!-- -->
             </span>
             <span class="spoiler-text">
              which is when something happens to a population that changes the genetic variation heavily. For example 50% of the reindeer population deing in an avalanche. Regardless if a reindeer has better traits to survive than the rest, the chances of it dieing is just as high.
             </span>
            </span>
            <br/>
            <br/>
            I'm sorry this sounds a bit weird, but I never learned the biological terms in English.
           </span>
          </div>
         </div>

          <div class="t withAv">
           Artificial doesn't exist in evolution. There was a niche that needed to be filled in an ecosystem that involved humans. Wolves that fit that niche came in and, well, filled the niche. The humans bred the animals to better fit the niches, which is survival of the fittest: The best animal to fit the niche got to reproduce, the bad ones didn't. People don't realize that humans, and everything they do, is part of nature.
          </div>

          <div class="t">
           Evolution just means the changing of organisms over time, so you're right, it is definitely evolution. The problem is when most people say "evolution" what they actually mean is "evolution through the process of natural selection", which the changing of wolves to dogs would NOT be, because there was nothing natural about that selection. When Iovot said that dogs didn't evolve from wolves, he most likely meant "evolution" in the latter sense, and he would be correct.
          </div>
         </div>

          <div style="clear:right">

          <div class="t">
           I'd call it breeding rather than evolution, I suppose the difference is minimal, if there's any at all now that I think about it. But still, I would hesitate to call it evolution seeing as dogs and wolves are still fundamentally the same animal, some of them might look radically different but genetically speaking the difference is minute, and they have no problem breeding together. The destination between wolfs and dogs is as contrived by humans as dogs themselves, we think of them as being different and in alot of ways they are, but in alot of ways a jack russel terrier is different than a saint bernard, wolves are unquestionably unique among their dog brethren but they're really more similar to a separate breed than a separate animal altogether.
          </div>
         </div>
        </div>
